NORTHANGER ABBEY

by

Jane Austen -LRB-1803-RRB-

ADVERTISEMENT BY THE AUTHORESS, TO NORTHANGER ABBEY

THIS little work was finished in the year 1803, and intended for immediate publication. It was disposed of to a bookseller, it was even advertised, and why the business proceeded no farther, the author has never been able to learn. That any bookseller should think it worth-while to purchase what he (author) did not think it worth-while to publish seems extraordinary. But with this, neither the author nor the public have any other concern than as some observation is necessary upon those parts of the work which thirteen years have made comparatively obsolete. The public are entreated to bear in mind that thirteen years have passed since it was finished, many more since it was begun, and that during that period, places, manners, books, and opinions have undergone considerable changes.

CHAPTER 1

No one who had ever seen Catherine Morland in her (Morland) infancy would have supposed her (Morland) born to be an heroine. Her (Morland) situation in life, the character of her (Morland) father and mother, her (Morland) own person and disposition, were all equally against her (Morland). Her (Morland) father was a clergyman, without being neglected, or poor, and a very respectable man, though his (man) name was Richard--and he (man) had never been handsome. He (man) had a considerable independence besides two good livings--and he (man) was not in the least addicted to locking up his (man) daughters. Her (Morland) mother was a woman of useful plain sense, with a good temper, and, what is more remarkable, with a good constitution. She (Morland) had three sons before Catherine was born; and instead of dying in bringing the latter into the world, as anybody might expect, she (Catherine) still lived on--lived to have six children more--to see them growing up around her (Catherine), and to enjoy excellent health herself. A family of ten children will be always called a fine family, where there are heads and arms and legs enough for the number; but the Morlands had little other right to the word, for they were in general very plain, and Catherine, for many years of her (Catherine) life, as plain as any. She (Catherine) had a thin awkward figure, a sallow skin without colour, dark lank hair, and strong features--so much for her (Catherine) person; and not less unpropitious for heroism seemed her (Catherine) mind. She (Catherine) was fond of all boy's plays, and greatly preferred cricket not merely to dolls, but to the more heroic enjoyments of infancy, nursing a dormouse, feeding a canary-bird, or watering a rose-bush. Indeed she (Catherine) had no taste for a garden; and if she (Catherine) gathered flowers at all, it was chiefly for the pleasure of mischief--at least so it was conjectured from her (Catherine) always preferring those which she (Catherine) was forbidden to take. Such were her (Catherine) propensities--her (Catherine) abilities were quite as extraordinary. She (Catherine) never could learn or understand anything before she (Catherine) was taught; and sometimes not even then, for she (Catherine) was often inattentive, and occasionally stupid. Her (Catherine) mother was three months in teaching her (Catherine) only to repeat the

``Beggar's Petition'' (mother)
; and after all, her (Catherine) next sister, Sally, could say it better than she (Catherine) did. Not that Catherine was always stupid--by no means; she (Catherine) learnt the fable of
``The Hare and Many Friends'' (she)
as quickly as any girl in England. Her (Catherine) mother wished her (Catherine) to learn music; and Catherine was sure she (Catherine) should like it, for she (Catherine) was very fond of tinkling the keys of the old forlorn spinnet; so, at eight years old she (Catherine) began. She (Catherine) learnt a year, and could not bear it; and Mrs. Morland, who did not insist on her (Morland) daughters being accomplished in spite of incapacity or distaste, allowed her (Morland) to leave off. The day which dismissed the music-master was one of the happiest of Catherine's life. Her (Catherine) taste for drawing was not superior; though whenever she (Catherine) could obtain the outside of a letter from her (Catherine) mother or seize upon any other odd piece of paper, she (Catherine) did what she (Catherine) could in that way, by drawing houses and trees, hens and chickens, all very much like one another. Writing and accounts she (Catherine) was taught by her (Catherine) father; French by her (Catherine) mother: her (Catherine) proficiency in either was not remarkable, and she (Catherine) shirked her (Catherine) lessons in both whenever she (Catherine) could. What a strange, unaccountable character!--for with all these symptoms of profligacy at ten years old, she (Catherine) had neither a bad heart nor a bad temper, was seldom stubborn, scarcely ever quarrelsome, and very kind to the little ones, with few interruptions of tyranny; she (Catherine) was moreover noisy and wild, hated confinement and cleanliness, and loved nothing so well in the world as rolling down the green slope at the back of the house.

Such was Catherine Morland at ten. At fifteen, appearances were mending; she (Morland) began to curl her (Morland) hair and long for balls; her (Morland) complexion improved, her (Morland) features were softened by plumpness and colour, her (Morland) eyes gained more animation, and her (Morland) figure more consequence. Her (Morland) love of dirt gave way to an inclination for finery, and she (Morland) grew clean as she (Morland) grew smart; she (Morland) had now the pleasure of sometimes hearing her (Morland) father and mother remark on her (Morland) personal improvement.

``Catherine grows quite a good-looking girl--she (Catherine) is almost pretty today,'' (her)
were words which caught her (Morland) ears now and then; and how welcome were the sounds! To look almost pretty is an acquisition of higher delight to a girl who has been looking plain the first fifteen years of her (girl) life than a beauty from her (girl) cradle can ever receive.

Mrs. Morland was a very good woman, and wished to see her (Morland) children everything they ought to be; but her (Morland) time was so much occupied in lying-in and teaching the little ones, that her (Morland) elder daughters were inevitably left to shift for themselves; and it was not very wonderful that Catherine, who had by nature nothing heroic about her (Catherine), should prefer cricket, baseball, riding on horseback, and running about the country at the age of fourteen, to books--or at least books of information--for, provided that nothing like useful knowledge could be gained from them, provided they were all story and no reflection, she (Catherine) had never any objection to books at all. But from fifteen to seventeen she (Catherine) was in training for a heroine; she (Catherine) read all such works as heroines must read to supply their memories with those quotations which are so serviceable and so soothing in the vicissitudes of their eventful lives.

From Pope, she (Catherine) learnt to censure those who

``bear about the mockery of woe.'' (she)

From Gray, that

``Many a flower is born to blush unseen,

``And waste its fragrance on the desert air.'' (Gray)

From Thompson, that--

``It is a delightful task

``To teach the young idea how to shoot.'' (Thompson that)

And from Shakespeare she (Catherine) gained a great store of information--amongst the rest, that--

``Trifles light as air, ``Are, to the jealous, confirmation strong,

``As proofs of Holy Writ.'' (she)

That

``The poor beetle, which we tread upon, ``In corporal sufferance feels a pang as great

``As when a giant dies.'' (a woman)

And that a young woman in love always looks--

``like Patience on a monument

``Smiling at Grief.'' (a woman)

So far her (woman) improvement was sufficient--and in many other points she (woman) came on exceedingly well; for though she (woman) could not write sonnets, she (woman) brought herself to read them; and though there seemed no chance of her (woman) throwing a whole party into raptures by a prelude on the pianoforte, of her (woman) own composition, she (woman) could listen to other people's performance with very little fatigue. Her (woman) greatest deficiency was in the pencil--she (woman) had no notion of drawing--not enough even to attempt a sketch of her (woman) lover's profile, that she (woman) might be detected in the design. There she (woman) fell miserably short of the true heroic height. At present she (woman) did not know her (woman) own poverty, for she (woman) had no lover to portray. She (woman) had reached the age of seventeen, without having seen one amiable youth who could call forth her (woman) sensibility, without having inspired one real passion, and without having excited even any admiration but what was very moderate and very transient. This was strange indeed! But strange things may be generally accounted for if their cause be fairly searched out. There was not one lord in the neighbourhood; no--not even a baronet. There was not one family among their acquaintance who had reared and supported a boy accidentally found at their door--not one young man whose origin was unknown. Her (woman) father had no ward, and the squire of the parish no children.

But when a young lady is to be a heroine, the perverseness of forty surrounding families cannot prevent her (heroine). Something must and will happen to throw a hero in her (heroine) way.

Mr. Allen, who owned the chief of the property about Fullerton, the village in Wiltshire where the Morlands lived, was ordered to Bath for the benefit of a gouty constitution--and his (Allen) lady, a good-humoured woman, fond of Miss Morland, and probably aware that if adventures will not befall a young lady in her (lady) own village, she (lady) must seek them abroad, invited her (lady) to go with them. Mr. and Mrs. Morland were all compliance, and Catherine all happiness.

CHAPTER 2

In addition to what has been already said of Catherine Morland's personal and mental endowments, when about to be launched into all the difficulties and dangers of a six weeks' residence in Bath, it may be stated, for the reader's more certain information, lest the following pages should otherwise fail of giving any idea of what her (Morland) character is meant to be, that her (Morland) heart was affectionate; her (Morland) disposition cheerful and open, without conceit or affectation of any kind--her (Morland) manners just removed from the awkwardness and shyness of a girl; her (Morland) person pleasing, and, when in good looks, pretty--and her (Morland) mind about as ignorant and uninformed as the female mind at seventeen usually is.

When the hour of departure drew near, the maternal anxiety of Mrs. Morland will be naturally supposed to be most severe. A thousand alarming presentiments of evil to her (Morland) beloved Catherine from this terrific separation must oppress her (Morland) heart with sadness, and drown her (Morland) in tears for the last day or two of their being together; and advice of the most important and applicable nature must of course flow from her (Morland) wise lips in their parting conference in her (Morland) closet. Cautions against the violence of such noblemen and baronets as delight in forcing young ladies away to some remote farm-house, must, at such a moment, relieve the fulness of her (Morland) heart. Who would not think so? But Mrs. Morland knew so little of lords and baronets, that she (Morland) entertained no notion of their general mischievousness, and was wholly unsuspicious of danger to her (Morland) daughter from their machinations. Her (Morland) cautions were confined to the following points.

``I beg, Catherine, you will always wrap yourself up very warm about the throat, when you come from the rooms at night; and I wish you would try to keep some account of the money you spend; I will give you this little book on purpose.'' (Sally)

Sally, or rather Sarah -LRB-for what young lady of common gentility will reach the age of sixteen without altering her (Sally) name as far as she (Sally) can?-RRB-, must from situation be at this time the intimate friend and confidante of her (Sally) sister. It is remarkable, however, that she (Sally) neither insisted on Catherine's writing by every post, nor exacted her (Catherine) promise of transmitting the character of every new acquaintance, nor a detail of every interesting conversation that Bath might produce. Everything indeed relative to this important journey was done, on the part of the Morlands, with a degree of moderation and composure, which seemed rather consistent with the common feelings of common life, than with the refined susceptibilities, the tender emotions which the first separation of a heroine from her (heroine) family ought always to excite. Her (heroine) father, instead of giving her (heroine) an unlimited order on his (father) banker, or even putting an hundred pounds bank-bill into her (heroine) hands, gave her (heroine) only ten guineas, and promised her (heroine) more when she (heroine) wanted it.

Under these unpromising auspices, the parting took place, and the journey began. It was performed with suitable quietness and uneventful safety. Neither robbers nor tempests befriended them, nor one lucky overturn to introduce them to the hero. Nothing more alarming occurred than a fear, on Mrs. Allen's side, of having once left her (Allen) clogs behind her (Allen) at an inn, and that fortunately proved to be groundless.

They arrived at Bath. Catherine was all eager delight--her (Catherine) eyes were here, there, everywhere, as they approached its fine and striking environs, and afterwards drove through those streets which conducted them to the hotel. She (Catherine) was come to be happy, and she (Catherine) felt happy already.

They were soon settled in comfortable lodgings in Pulteney Street.

It is now expedient to give some description of Mrs. Allen, that the reader may be able to judge in what manner her (Allen) actions will hereafter tend to promote the general distress of the work, and how she (Allen) will, probably, contribute to reduce poor Catherine to all the desperate wretchedness of which a last volume is capable--whether by her (Catherine) imprudence, vulgarity, or jealousy--whether by intercepting her (Catherine) letters, ruining her (Catherine) character, or turning her (Catherine) out of doors.

Mrs. Allen was one of that numerous class of females, whose society can raise no other emotion than surprise at there being any men in the world who could like them well enough to marry them. She (Allen) had neither beauty, genius, accomplishment, nor manner. The air of a gentlewoman, a great deal of quiet, inactive good temper, and a trifling turn of mind were all that could account for her (Allen) being the choice of a sensible, intelligent man like Mr. Allen. In one respect she (Allen) was admirably fitted to introduce a young lady into public, being as fond of going everywhere and seeing everything herself as any young lady could be. Dress was her (lady) passion. She (lady) had a most harmless delight in being fine; and our heroine's entree into life could not take place till after three or four days had been spent in learning what was mostly worn, and her (lady) chaperone was provided with a dress of the newest fashion. Catherine too made some purchases herself, and when all these matters were arranged, the important evening came which was to usher her (Catherine) into the Upper Rooms. Her (Catherine) hair was cut and dressed by the best hand, her (Catherine) clothes put on with care, and both Mrs. Allen and her (Allen) maid declared she (Allen) looked quite as she (Allen) should do. With such encouragement, Catherine hoped at least to pass uncensured through the crowd. As for admiration, it was always very welcome when it came, but she (Catherine) did not depend on it.

Mrs. Allen was so long in dressing that they did not enter the ballroom till late. The season was full, the room crowded, and the two ladies squeezed in as well as they could. As for Mr. Allen, he (Allen) repaired directly to the card-room, and left them to enjoy a mob by themselves. With more care for the safety of her (Allen) new gown than for the comfort of her (Allen) protegee, Mrs. Allen made her (Allen) way through the throng of men by the door, as swiftly as the necessary caution would allow; Catherine, however, kept close at her (Catherine) side, and linked her (Catherine) arm too firmly within her (Catherine) friend's to be torn asunder by any common effort of a struggling assembly. But to her (Catherine) utter amazement she (Catherine) found that to proceed along the room was by no means the way to disengage themselves from the crowd; it seemed rather to increase as they went on, whereas she (Catherine) had imagined that when once fairly within the door, they should easily find seats and be able to watch the dances with perfect convenience. But this was far from being the case, and though by unwearied diligence they gained even the top of the room, their situation was just the same; they saw nothing of the dancers but the high feathers of some of the ladies. Still they moved on--something better was yet in view; and by a continued exertion of strength and ingenuity they found themselves at last in the passage behind the highest bench. Here there was something less of crowd than below; and hence Miss Morland had a comprehensive view of all the company beneath her (Morland), and of all the dangers of her (Morland) late passage through them. It was a splendid sight, and she (Morland) began, for the first time that evening, to feel herself at a ball: she (Morland) longed to dance, but she (Morland) had not an acquaintance in the room. Mrs. Allen did all that she (Allen) could do in such a case by saying very placidly, every now and then,

``I wish you could dance, my dear--I wish you could get a partner.'' (she)
For some time her (Allen) young friend felt obliged to her (Allen) for these wishes; but they were repeated so often, and proved so totally ineffectual, that Catherine grew tired at last, and would thank her (Catherine) no more.

They were not long able, however, to enjoy the repose of the eminence they had so laboriously gained. Everybody was shortly in motion for tea, and they must squeeze out like the rest. Catherine began to feel something of disappointment--she (Catherine) was tired of being continually pressed against by people, the generality of whose faces possessed nothing to interest, and with all of whom she (Catherine) was so wholly unacquainted that she (Catherine) could not relieve the irksomeness of imprisonment by the exchange of a syllable with any of her (Catherine) fellow captives; and when at last arrived in the tea-room, she (Catherine) felt yet more the awkwardness of having no party to join, no acquaintance to claim, no gentleman to assist them. They saw nothing of Mr. Allen; and after looking about them in vain for a more eligible situation, were obliged to sit down at the end of a table, at which a large party were already placed, without having anything to do there, or anybody to speak to, except each other.

Mrs. Allen congratulated herself, as soon as they were seated, on having preserved her (Allen) gown from injury.

She (Catherine) was looked at, however, and with some admiration; for, in her (Catherine) own hearing, two gentlemen pronounced her (Catherine) to be a pretty girl. CHAPTER 3

Every morning now brought its regular duties--shops were to be visited; some new part of the town to be looked at; and the pump-room to be attended, where they paraded up and down for an hour, looking at everybody and speaking to no one. The wish of a numerous acquaintance in Bath was still uppermost with Mrs. Allen, and she (Allen) repeated it after every fresh proof, which every morning brought, of her (Allen) knowing nobody at all.

They made their appearance in the Lower Rooms; and here fortune was more favourable to our heroine. The master of the ceremonies introduced to her (heroine) a very gentlemanlike young man as a partner; his (man) name was Tilney. He (man) seemed to be about four or five and twenty, was rather tall, had a pleasing countenance, a very intelligent and lively eye, and, if not quite handsome, was very near it. His (man) address was good, and Catherine felt herself in high luck. There was little leisure for speaking while they danced; but when they were seated at tea, she (Catherine) found him (man) as agreeable as she (Catherine) had already given him (man) credit for being. He (man) talked with fluency and spirit--and there was an archness and pleasantry in his (man) manner which interested, though it was hardly understood by her (Catherine). ``I have hitherto been very remiss, madam, in the proper attentions of a partner here; I have not yet asked you how long you have been in Bath; whether you were ever here before; whether you have been at the Upper Rooms, the theatre, and the concert; and how you like the place altogether. I have been very negligent--but are you now at leisure to satisfy me in these particulars? If you are I will begin directly.'' (her)

``You need not give yourself that trouble, sir.'' (unknown)

``Perhaps you are not sitting in this room, and I am not sitting by you. These are points in which a doubt is equally possible. Not keep a journal! How are your absent cousins to understand the tenour of your life in Bath without one? How are the civilities and compliments of every day to be related as they ought to be, unless noted down every evening in a journal? How are your various dresses to be remembered, and the particular state of your complexion, and curl of your hair to be described in all their diversities, without having constant recourse to a journal? My dear madam, I am not so ignorant of young ladies' ways as you wish to believe me; it is this delightful habit of journaling which largely contributes to form the easy style of writing for which ladies are so generally celebrated. Everybody allows that the talent of writing agreeable letters is peculiarly female. Nature may have done something, but I am sure it must be essentially assisted by the practice of keeping a journal.'' (unknown)

``I have sometimes thought,'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

``whether ladies do write so much better letters than gentlemen! That is--I should not think the superiority was always on our side.'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

CHAPTER 4

Every creature in Bath, except himself, was to be seen in the room at different periods of the fashionable hours; crowds of people were every moment passing in and out, up the steps and down; people whom nobody cared about, and nobody wanted to see; and he (Tilney) only was absent.

This brief account of the family is intended to supersede the necessity of a long and minute detail from Mrs. Thorpe herself, of her (Thorpe) past adventures and sufferings, which might otherwise be expected to occupy the three or four following chapters; in which the worthlessness of lords and attorneys might be set forth, and conversations, which had passed twenty years before, be minutely repeated.

CHAPTER 5

The progress of the friendship between Catherine and Isabella was quick as its beginning had been warm, and they passed so rapidly through every gradation of increasing tenderness that there was shortly no fresh proof of it to be given to their friends or themselves. They called each other by their Christian name, were always arm in arm when they walked, pinned up each other's train for the dance, and were not to be divided in the set; and if a rainy morning deprived them of other enjoyments, they were still resolute in meeting in defiance of wet and dirt, and shut themselves up, to read novels together. Yes, novels; for I will not adopt that ungenerous and impolitic custom so common with novel-writers, of degrading by their contemptuous censure the very performances, to the number of which they are themselves adding--joining with their greatest enemies in bestowing the harshest epithets on such works, and scarcely ever permitting them to be read by their own heroine, who, if she (heroine) accidentally take up a novel, is sure to turn over its insipid pages with disgust. Alas! If the heroine of one novel be not patronized by the heroine of another, from whom can she (heroine) expect protection and regard? I cannot approve of it. Let us leave it to the reviewers to abuse such effusions of fancy at their leisure, and over every new novel to talk in threadbare strains of the trash with which the press now groans. Let us not desert one another; we are an injured body. Although our productions have afforded more extensive and unaffected pleasure than those of any other literary corporation in the world, no species of composition has been so much decried. From pride, ignorance, or fashion, our foes are almost as many as our readers. And while the abilities of the nine-hundredth abridger of the History of England, or of the man who collects and publishes in a volume some dozen lines of Milton, Pope, and Prior, with a paper from the Spectator, and a chapter from Sterne, are eulogized by a thousand pens--there seems almost a general wish of decrying the capacity and undervaluing the labour of the novelist, and of slighting the performances which have only genius, wit, and taste to recommend them.

``I am no novel-reader--I seldom look into novels--Do not imagine that I often read novels--It is really very well for a novel.'' (unknown)
Such is the common cant.
``And what are you reading, Miss--?'' (unknown)
``Oh! It is only a novel!'' (the lady)
replies the young lady, while she (lady) lays down her (lady) book with affected indifference, or momentary shame.
``It is only Cecilia, or Camilla, or Belinda'' (unknown)
; or, in short, only some work in which the greatest powers of the mind are displayed, in which the most thorough knowledge of human nature, the happiest delineation of its varieties, the liveliest effusions of wit and humour, are conveyed to the world in the best-chosen language. Now, had the same young lady been engaged with a volume of the Spectator, instead of such a work, how proudly would she (lady) have produced the book, and told its name; though the chances must be against her (lady) being occupied by any part of that voluminous publication, of which either the matter or manner would not disgust a young person of taste: the substance of its papers so often consisting in the statement of improbable circumstances, unnatural characters, and topics of conversation which no longer concern anyone living; and their language, too, frequently so coarse as to give no very favourable idea of the age that could endure it.

CHAPTER 6

The following conversation, which took place between the two friends in the pump-room one morning, after an acquaintance of eight or nine days, is given as a specimen of their very warm attachment, and of the delicacy, discretion, originality of thought, and literary taste which marked the reasonableness of that attachment.

CHAPTER 7

Half a minute conducted them through the pump-yard to the archway, opposite Union Passage; but here they were stopped. Everybody acquainted with Bath may remember the difficulties of crossing Cheap Street at this point; it is indeed a street of so impertinent a nature, so unfortunately connected with the great London and Oxford roads, and the principal inn of the city, that a day never passes in which parties of ladies, however important their business, whether in quest of pastry, millinery, or even -LRB-as in the present case-RRB- of young men, are not detained on one side or other by carriages, horsemen, or carts. ``Oh, these odious gigs!'' (Isabella/Thorpe/Isabella Thorpe)

Catherine had fortitude too; she (Catherine) suffered, but no murmur passed her (Catherine) lips.

Dress is at all times a frivolous distinction, and excessive solicitude about it often destroys its own aim. Catherine knew all this very well; her (Catherine) great aunt had read her (Catherine) a lecture on the subject only the Christmas before; and yet she (Catherine) lay awake ten minutes on Wednesday night debating between her (Catherine) spotted and her (Catherine) tamboured muslin, and nothing but the shortness of the time prevented her (Catherine) buying a new one for the evening. This would have been an error in judgment, great though not uncommon, from which one of the other sex rather than her (Catherine) own, a brother rather than a great aunt, might have warned her (Catherine), for man only can be aware of the insensibility of man towards a new gown. It would be mortifying to the feelings of many ladies, could they be made to understand how little the heart of man is affected by what is costly or new in their attire; how little it is biased by the texture of their muslin, and how unsusceptible of peculiar tenderness towards the spotted, the sprigged, the mull, or the jackonet. Woman is fine for her (Woman) own satisfaction alone. No man will admire her (Woman) the more, no woman will like her (Woman) the better for it. Neatness and fashion are enough for the former, and a something of shabbiness or impropriety will be most endearing to the latter. But not one of these grave reflections troubled the tranquillity of Catherine.

Blaize Castle remained her (Catherine) only comfort; towards that, she (Catherine) still looked at intervals with pleasure; though rather than be disappointed of the promised walk, and especially rather than be thought ill of by the Tilneys, she (Catherine) would willingly have given up all the happiness which its walls could supply--the happiness of a progress through a long suite of lofty rooms, exhibiting the remains of magnificent furniture, though now for many years deserted--the happiness of being stopped in their way along narrow, winding vaults, by a low, grated door; or even of having their lamp, their only lamp, extinguished by a sudden gust of wind, and of being left in total darkness. In the meanwhile, they proceeded on their journey without any mischance, and were within view of the town of Keynsham, when a halloo from Morland, who was behind them, made his (their) friend pull up, to know what was the matter. The others then came close enough for conversation, and Morland said,

``We had better go back, Thorpe; it is too late to go on today; your sister thinks so as well as I. We had much better put it off till another day, and turn round.'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

``It is all one to me,'' (Isabella/Thorpe/Isabella Thorpe)

``If your brother had not got such a d--beast to drive,'' (he)

``No, he (fool) is not,'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

``for I am sure he (fool) could not afford it.'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

``And why cannot he (fool) afford it?'' (unknown)

``Because he (fool) has not money enough.'' (unknown)

``And whose fault is that?'' (unknown)

``Nobody's, that I know of.'' (Isabella/Thorpe/Isabella Thorpe)
Thorpe then said something in the loud, incoherent way to which he (their) had often recourse, about its being a d--thing to be miserly; and that if people who rolled in money could not afford things, he (their) did not know who could, which Catherine did not even endeavour to understand. Pondering over these heart-rending tidings, Catherine walked slowly upstairs. At the head of them she (Catherine) was met by Mr. Allen, who, on hearing the reason of their speedy return, said,

``I am glad your brother had so much sense; I am glad you are come back. It was a strange, wild scheme.'' (Mr. Allen)

They all spent the evening together at Thorpe's. Catherine was disturbed and out of spirits; but Isabella seemed to find a pool of commerce, in the fate of which she (Isabella) shared, by private partnership with Morland, a very good equivalent for the quiet and country air of an inn at Clifton. Her (Morland) satisfaction, too, in not being at the Lower Rooms was spoken more than once.

``How I pity the poor creatures that are going there! How glad I am that I am not amongst them! I wonder whether it will be a full ball or not! They have not begun dancing yet. I would not be there for all the world. It is so delightful to have an evening now and then to oneself. I dare say it will not be a very good ball. I know the Mitchells will not be there. I am sure I pity everybody that is. But I dare say, Mr. Morland, you long to be at it, do not you? I am sure you do. Well, pray do not let anybody here be a restraint on you. I dare say we could do very well without you; but you men think yourselves of such consequence.'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

Catherine could almost have accused Isabella of being wanting in tenderness towards herself and her (Catherine) sorrows, so very little did they appear to dwell on her (Catherine) mind, and so very inadequate was the comfort she (Catherine) offered.

``Do not be so dull, my dearest creature,'' (she)
she (Catherine) whispered.
``You will quite break my heart. It was amazingly shocking, to be sure; but the Tilneys were entirely to blame. Why were not they more punctual? It was dirty, indeed, but what did that signify? I am sure John and I should not have minded it. I never mind going through anything, where a friend is concerned; that is my disposition, and John is just the same; he (John) has amazing strong feelings. Good heavens! What a delightful hand you have got! Kings, I vow! I never was so happy in my life! I would fifty times rather you should have them than myself.'' (I)

And now I may dismiss my heroine to the sleepless couch, which is the true heroine's portion; to a pillow strewed with thorns and wet with tears. And lucky may she (heroine) think herself, if she (heroine) get another good night's rest in the course of the next three months.

CHAPTER 12

``Mrs. Allen,'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)
said Catherine the next morning,
``will there be any harm in my calling on Miss Tilney today? I shall not be easy till I have explained everything.'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

``Go, by all means, my dear; only put on a white gown; Miss Tilney always wears white.'' (Catherine/Morland/Catherine Morland)

Catherine cheerfully complied, and being properly equipped, was more impatient than ever to be at the pump-room, that she (Catherine) might inform herself of General Tilney's lodgings, for though she (Catherine) believed they were in Milsom Street, she (Catherine) was not certain of the house, and Mrs. Allen's wavering convictions only made it more doubtful. She (Allen) reached the house without any impediment, looked at the number, knocked at the door, and inquired for Miss Tilney. The man believed Miss Tilney to be at home, but was not quite certain. She (Catherine) knew not how such an offence as hers might be classed by the laws of worldly politeness, to what a degree of unforgivingness it might with propriety lead, nor to what rigours of rudeness in return it might justly make her (Catherine) amenable.

Dejected and humbled, she (Catherine) had even some thoughts of not going with the others to the theatre that night; but it must be confessed that they were not of long continuance, for she (Catherine) soon recollected, in the first place, that she (Catherine) was without any excuse for staying at home; and, in the second, that it was a play she (Catherine) wanted very much to see. To the theatre accordingly they all went; no Tilneys appeared to plague or please her (Catherine); she (Catherine) feared that, amongst the many perfections of the family, a fondness for plays was not to be ranked; but perhaps it was because they were habituated to the finer performances of the London stage, which she (Catherine) knew, on Isabella's authority, rendered everything else of the kind

``quite horrid.'' (Isabella/Thorpe/Isabella Thorpe)
She (Catherine) was not deceived in her (Catherine) own expectation of pleasure; the comedy so well suspended her (Catherine) care that no one, observing her (Catherine) during the first four acts, would have supposed she (Catherine) had any wretchedness about her (Catherine). On the beginning of the fifth, however, the sudden view of Mr. Henry Tilney and his (Tilney) father, joining a party in the opposite box, recalled her (Catherine) to anxiety and distress. CHAPTER 13

Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday, Friday, and Saturday have now passed in review before the reader; the events of each day, its hopes and fears, mortifications and pleasures, have been separately stated, and the pangs of Sunday only now remain to be described, and close the week. The Clifton scheme had been deferred, not relinquished, and on the afternoon's Crescent of this day, it was brought forward again. In a private consultation between Isabella and James, the former of whom had particularly set her (Isabella) heart upon going, and the latter no less anxiously placed his (James) upon pleasing her (Isabella), it was agreed that, provided the weather were fair, the party should take place on the following morning; and they were to set off very early, in order to be at home in good time. The affair thus determined, and Thorpe's approbation secured, Catherine only remained to be apprised of it. She (Catherine) had left them for a few minutes to speak to Miss Tilney. In that interval the plan was completed, and as soon as she (Tilney) came again, her (Tilney) agreement was demanded; but instead of the gay acquiescence expected by Isabella, Catherine looked grave, was very sorry, but could not go. The engagement which ought to have kept her (Catherine) from joining in the former attempt would make it impossible for her (Catherine) to accompany them now. She (Catherine) had that moment settled with Miss Tilney to take their proposed walk tomorrow; it was quite determined, and she (Tilney) would not, upon any account, retract. But that she (Tilney) must and should retract was instantly the eager cry of both the Thorpes; they must go to Clifton tomorrow, they would not go without her (Tilney), it would be nothing to put off a mere walk for one day longer, and they would not hear of a refusal. Catherine was distressed, but not subdued.

``Do not urge me, Isabella. I am engaged to Miss Tilney. I cannot go.'' (unknown)
This availed nothing. The same arguments assailed her (Catherine) again; she (Catherine) must go, she (Catherine) should go, and they would not hear of a refusal.
``It would be so easy to tell Miss Tilney that you had just been reminded of a prior engagement, and must only beg to put off the walk till Tuesday.'' (unknown)

``No, it would not be easy. I could not do it. There has been no prior engagement.'' (Isabella/Thorpe/Isabella Thorpe)

She (Catherine) knew her (Catherine) beloved Catherine to have so feeling a heart, so sweet a temper, to be so easily persuaded by those she (Catherine) loved. ``I cannot help being jealous, Catherine, when I see myself slighted for strangers, I, who love you so excessively! When once my affections are placed, it is not in the power of anything to change them. ``Let her (Isabella) go, let her (Isabella) go, if she (Isabella) will go.'' (unknown)

CHAPTER 15

CHAPTER 23

``This lengthened absence, these solitary rambles, did not speak a mind at ease, or a conscience void of reproach.'' (he)

With the walls of the kitchen ended all the antiquity of the abbey; the fourth side of the quadrangle having, on account of its decaying state, been removed by the general's father, and the present erected in its place. CHAPTER 24

The next day afforded no opportunity for the proposed examination of the mysterious apartments. Murder was not tolerated, servants were not slaves, and neither poison nor sleeping potions to be procured, like rhubarb, from every druggist. Among the Alps and Pyrenees, perhaps, there were no mixed characters. There, such as were not as spotless as an angel might have the dispositions of a fiend. If Wednesday should ever come!

It did come, and exactly when it might be reasonably looked for. It came--it was fine--and Catherine trod on air. By ten o’clock, the chaise and four conveyed the trio from the abbey; and, after an agreeable drive of almost twenty miles, they entered Woodston, a large and populous village, in a situation not unpleasant. Catherine was ashamed to say how pretty she (Catherine) thought it, as the general seemed to think an apology necessary for the flatness of the country, and the size of the village; but in her (Catherine) heart she (Catherine) preferred it to any place she (Catherine) had ever been at, and looked with great admiration at every neat house above the rank of a cottage, and at all the little chandler's shops which they passed. At the further end of the village, and tolerably disengaged from the rest of it, stood the parsonage, a new-built substantial stone house, with its semicircular sweep and green gates; and, as they drove up to the door, Henry, with the friends of his (Henry) solitude, a large Newfoundland puppy and two or three terriers, was ready to receive and make much of them.

Catherine's mind was too full, as she (Catherine) entered the house, for her (Catherine) either to observe or to say a great deal; and, till called on by the general for her (Catherine) opinion of it, she (Catherine) had very little idea of the room in which she (Catherine) was sitting. ``We are not calling it a good house,'' (he)

She (Catherine) could not but observe that the abundance of the dinner did not seem to create the smallest astonishment in the general; nay, that he (Catherine) was even looking at the side-table for cold meat which was not there. CHAPTER 27

The next morning brought the following very unexpected letter from Isabella:

Bath, April

My dearest Catherine, I received your two kind letters with the greatest delight, and have a thousand apologies to make for not answering them sooner. I really am quite ashamed of my idleness; but in this horrid place one can find time for nothing. I have had my pen in my hand to begin a letter to you almost every day since you left Bath, but have always been prevented by some silly trifler or other. Pray write to me soon, and direct to my own home. Thank God, we leave this vile place tomorrow. Since you went away, I have had no pleasure in it--the dust is beyond anything; and everybody one cares for is gone. I believe if I could see you I should not mind the rest, for you are dearer to me than anybody can conceive. I am quite uneasy about your dear brother, not having heard from him (brother) since he (brother) went to Oxford; and am fearful of some misunderstanding. Your kind offices will set all right: he (brother) is the only man I ever did or could love, and I trust you will convince him (brother) of it. The spring fashions are partly down; and the hats the most frightful you can imagine. I hope you spend your time pleasantly, but am afraid you never think of me. I will not say all that I could of the family you are with, because I would not be ungenerous, or set you against those you esteem; but it is very difficult to know whom to trust, and young men never know their minds two days together. I rejoice to say that the young man whom, of all others, I particularly abhor, has left Bath. You will know, from this description, I must mean Captain Tilney, who, as you may remember, was amazingly disposed to follow and tease me, before you went away. Afterwards he (Captain) got worse, and became quite my shadow. Many girls might have been taken in, for never were such attentions; but I knew the fickle sex too well. He (Captain) went away to his (Captain) regiment two days ago, and I trust I shall never be plagued with him (Captain) again. He (Captain) is the greatest coxcomb I ever saw, and amazingly disagreeable. The last two days he (Captain) was always by the side of Charlotte Davis: I pitied his (Davis) taste, but took no notice of him (Davis). The last time we met was in Bath Street, and I turned directly into a shop that he (Davis) might not speak to me; I would not even look at him (Davis). He (Davis) went into the pump-room afterwards; but I would not have followed him (Davis) for all the world. Such a contrast between him (Davis) and your brother! I would write to him (Davis) myself, but have mislaid his (Davis) direction; and, as I hinted above, am afraid he (Davis) took something in my conduct amiss. We happened to sit by the Mitchells, and they pretended to be quite surprised to see me out. I knew their spite: at one time they could not be civil to me, but now they are all friendship; but I am not such a fool as to be taken in by them. You know I have a pretty good spirit of my own. I wear nothing but purple now: I know I look hideous in it, but no matter--it is your dear brother's favourite colour. was information on Sarah's side, which produced only a bow of acknowledgment from the gentleman, and a silencing nod from her (Sarah) mother; for Mrs. Morland, thinking it probable, as a secondary consideration in his (gentleman) wish of waiting on their worthy neighbours, that he (gentleman) might have some explanation to give of his (gentleman) father's behaviour, which it must be more pleasant for him (gentleman) to communicate only to Catherine, would not on any account prevent her (Catherine) accompanying him (gentleman). They began their walk, and Mrs. Morland was not entirely mistaken in his (gentleman) object in wishing it. Some explanation on his (gentleman) father's account he (gentleman) had to give; but his (gentleman) first purpose was to explain himself, and before they reached Mr. Allen's grounds he (Allen) had done it so well that Catherine did not think it could ever be repeated too often. She (Catherine) was assured of his (Allen) affection; and that heart in return was solicited, which, perhaps, they pretty equally knew was already entirely his (Allen) own; for, though Henry was now sincerely attached to her (Catherine), though he (Henry) felt and delighted in all the excellencies of her (Catherine) character and truly loved her (Catherine) society, I must confess that his (Henry) affection originated in nothing better than gratitude, or, in other words, that a persuasion of her (Catherine) partiality for him (Henry) had been the only cause of giving her (Catherine) a serious thought. It is a new circumstance in romance, I acknowledge, and dreadfully derogatory of an heroine's dignity; but if it be as new in common life, the credit of a wild imagination will at least be all my own.

A very short visit to Mrs. Allen, in which Henry talked at random, without sense or connection, and Catherine, rapt in the contemplation of her (Catherine) own unutterable happiness, scarcely opened her (Catherine) lips, dismissed them to the ecstasies of another tete-a-tete; and before it was suffered to close, she (Catherine) was enabled to judge how far he (Henry) was sanctioned by parental authority in his (Henry) present application. On his (Henry) return from Woodston, two days before, he (Henry) had been met near the abbey by his (Henry) impatient father, hastily informed in angry terms of Miss Morland's departure, and ordered to think of her (Morland) no more.

Such was the permission upon which he (Henry) had now offered her (Morland) his (Henry) hand. The affrighted Catherine, amidst all the terrors of expectation, as she (Catherine) listened to this account, could not but rejoice in the kind caution with which Henry had saved her (Catherine) from the necessity of a conscientious rejection, by engaging her (Catherine) faith before he (Henry) mentioned the subject; and as he (Henry) proceeded to give the particulars, and explain the motives of his (Henry) father's conduct, her (Catherine) feelings soon hardened into even a triumphant delight. The general had had nothing to accuse her (Catherine) of, nothing to lay to her (Catherine) charge, but her (Catherine) being the involuntary, unconscious object of a deception which his (Henry) pride could not pardon, and which a better pride would have been ashamed to own. She (Catherine) was guilty only of being less rich than he (Henry) had supposed her (Catherine) to be. Under a mistaken persuasion of her (Catherine) possessions and claims, he (Henry) had courted her (Catherine) acquaintance in Bath, solicited her (Catherine) company at Northanger, and designed her (Catherine) for his (Henry) daughter-in-law. On discovering his (Henry) error, to turn her (Catherine) from the house seemed the best, though to his (Henry) feelings an inadequate proof of his (Henry) resentment towards herself, and his (Henry) contempt of her (Catherine) family.

John Thorpe had first misled him (Thorpe). The general, perceiving his (Thorpe) son one night at the theatre to be paying considerable attention to Miss Morland, had accidentally inquired of Thorpe if he (Thorpe) knew more of her (Thorpe) than her (Thorpe) name. Thorpe, most happy to be on speaking terms with a man of General Tilney's importance, had been joyfully and proudly communicative; and being at that time not only in daily expectation of Morland's engaging Isabella, but likewise pretty well resolved upon marrying Catherine himself, his (General) vanity induced him (General) to represent the family as yet more wealthy than his (General) vanity and avarice had made him (General) believe them. With whomsoever he (General) was, or was likely to be connected, his (General) own consequence always required that theirs should be great, and as his (General) intimacy with any acquaintance grew, so regularly grew their fortune. The expectations of his (General) friend Morland, therefore, from the first overrated, had ever since his (General) introduction to Isabella been gradually increasing; and by merely adding twice as much for the grandeur of the moment, by doubling what he (General) chose to think the amount of Mr. Morland's preferment, trebling his (Morland) private fortune, bestowing a rich aunt, and sinking half the children, he (Morland) was able to represent the whole family to the general in a most respectable light. For Catherine, however, the peculiar object of the general's curiosity, and his (general) own speculations, he (general) had yet something more in reserve, and the ten or fifteen thousand pounds which her (Catherine) father could give her (Catherine) would be a pretty addition to Mr. Allen's estate. Her (Catherine) intimacy there had made him (Allen) seriously determine on her (Catherine) being handsomely legacied hereafter; and to speak of her (Catherine) therefore as the almost acknowledged future heiress of Fullerton naturally followed. Upon such intelligence the general had proceeded; for never had it occurred to him (general) to doubt its authority. Thorpe's interest in the family, by his (general) sister's approaching connection with one of its members, and his (general) own views on another -LRB-circumstances of which he (general) boasted with almost equal openness-RRB-, seemed sufficient vouchers for his (general) truth; and to these were added the absolute facts of the Allens being wealthy and childless, of Miss Morland's being under their care, and--as soon as his (general) acquaintance allowed him (general) to judge--of their treating her (Morland) with parental kindness. His (general) resolution was soon formed. Already had he (general) discerned a liking towards Miss Morland in the countenance of his (general) son; and thankful for Mr. Thorpe's communication, he (Thorpe) almost instantly determined to spare no pains in weakening his (Thorpe) boasted interest and ruining his (Thorpe) dearest hopes. Catherine herself could not be more ignorant at the time of all this, than his (Thorpe) own children. Henry and Eleanor, perceiving nothing in her (Eleanor) situation likely to engage their father's particular respect, had seen with astonishment the suddenness, continuance, and extent of his (father) attention; and though latterly, from some hints which had accompanied an almost positive command to his (father) son of doing everything in his (father) power to attach her (Eleanor), Henry was convinced of his (Henry) father's believing it to be an advantageous connection, it was not till the late explanation at Northanger that they had the smallest idea of the false calculations which had hurried him (Henry) on. That they were false, the general had learnt from the very person who had suggested them, from Thorpe himself, whom he (Henry) had chanced to meet again in town, and who, under the influence of exactly opposite feelings, irritated by Catherine's refusal, and yet more by the failure of a very recent endeavour to accomplish a reconciliation between Morland and Isabella, convinced that they were separated forever, and spurning a friendship which could be no longer serviceable, hastened to contradict all that he (Morland) had said before to the advantage of the Morlands--confessed himself to have been totally mistaken in his (Morland) opinion of their circumstances and character, misled by the rhodomontade of his (Morland) friend to believe his (Morland) father a man of substance and credit, whereas the transactions of the two or three last weeks proved him (Morland) to be neither; for after coming eagerly forward on the first overture of a marriage between the families, with the most liberal proposals, he (Morland) had, on being brought to the point by the shrewdness of the relator, been constrained to acknowledge himself incapable of giving the young people even a decent support. They were, in fact, a necessitous family; numerous, too, almost beyond example; by no means respected in their own neighbourhood, as he (Morland) had lately had particular opportunities of discovering; aiming at a style of life which their fortune could not warrant; seeking to better themselves by wealthy connections; a forward, bragging, scheming race.

The terrified general pronounced the name of Allen with an inquiring look; and here too Thorpe had learnt his (Morland) error. The Allens, he (Morland) believed, had lived near them too long, and he (Morland) knew the young man on whom the Fullerton estate must devolve. The general needed no more. Enraged with almost everybody in the world but himself, he (Morland) set out the next day for the abbey, where his (Morland) performances have been seen.

I leave it to my reader's sagacity to determine how much of all this it was possible for Henry to communicate at this time to Catherine, how much of it he (Henry) could have learnt from his (Henry) father, in what points his (Henry) own conjectures might assist him (Henry), and what portion must yet remain to be told in a letter from James. I have united for their ease what they must divide for mine. Catherine, at any rate, heard enough to feel that in suspecting General Tilney of either murdering or shutting up his (General) wife, she (wife) had scarcely sinned against his (General) character, or magnified his (General) cruelty.

Henry, in having such things to relate of his (General) father, was almost as pitiable as in their first avowal to himself. He (General) blushed for the narrow-minded counsel which he (General) was obliged to expose. The conversation between them at Northanger had been of the most unfriendly kind. Henry's indignation on hearing how Catherine had been treated, on comprehending his (Henry) father's views, and being ordered to acquiesce in them, had been open and bold. The general, accustomed on every ordinary occasion to give the law in his (Henry) family, prepared for no reluctance but of feeling, no opposing desire that should dare to clothe itself in words, could ill brook the opposition of his (Henry) son, steady as the sanction of reason and the dictate of conscience could make it. But, in such a cause, his (Henry) anger, though it must shock, could not intimidate Henry, who was sustained in his (Henry) purpose by a conviction of its justice. He (Henry) felt himself bound as much in honour as in affection to Miss Morland, and believing that heart to be his (Henry) own which he (Henry) had been directed to gain, no unworthy retraction of a tacit consent, no reversing decree of unjustifiable anger, could shake his (Henry) fidelity, or influence the resolutions it prompted.

He (Henry) steadily refused to accompany his (Henry) father into Herefordshire, an engagement formed almost at the moment to promote the dismissal of Catherine, and as steadily declared his (Henry) intention of offering her (Catherine) his (Henry) hand. The general was furious in his (general) anger, and they parted in dreadful disagreement. Henry, in an agitation of mind which many solitary hours were required to compose, had returned almost instantly to Woodston, and, on the afternoon of the following day, had begun his (Henry) journey to Fullerton.

CHAPTER 31

Mr. and Mrs. Morland's surprise on being applied to by Mr. Tilney for their consent to his (Tilney) marrying their daughter was, for a few minutes, considerable, it having never entered their heads to suspect an attachment on either side; but as nothing, after all, could be more natural than Catherine's being beloved, they soon learnt to consider it with only the happy agitation of gratified pride, and, as far as they alone were concerned, had not a single objection to start. His (Tilney) pleasing manners and good sense were self-evident recommendations; and having never heard evil of him (Tilney), it was not their way to suppose any evil could be told. Goodwill supplying the place of experience, his (Tilney) character needed no attestation.

``Catherine would make a sad, heedless young housekeeper to be sure,'' (mother)
was her (Catherine) mother's foreboding remark; but quick was the consolation of there being nothing like practice.

There was but one obstacle, in short, to be mentioned; but till that one was removed, it must be impossible for them to sanction the engagement. Their tempers were mild, but their principles were steady, and while his (Tilney) parent so expressly forbade the connection, they could not allow themselves to encourage it. That the general should come forward to solicit the alliance, or that he (Tilney) should even very heartily approve it, they were not refined enough to make any parading stipulation; but the decent appearance of consent must be yielded, and that once obtained--and their own hearts made them trust that it could not be very long denied--their willing approbation was instantly to follow. His (Tilney) consent was all that they wished for. They were no more inclined than entitled to demand his (Tilney) money. Of a very considerable fortune, his (Tilney) son was, by marriage settlements, eventually secure; his (Tilney) present income was an income of independence and comfort, and under every pecuniary view, it was a match beyond the claims of their daughter.

The young people could not be surprised at a decision like this. They felt and they deplored--but they could not resent it; and they parted, endeavouring to hope that such a change in the general, as each believed almost impossible, might speedily take place, to unite them again in the fullness of privileged affection. Henry returned to what was now his (Henry) only home, to watch over his (Henry) young plantations, and extend his (Henry) improvements for her (daughter) sake, to whose share in them he (Henry) looked anxiously forward; and Catherine remained at Fullerton to cry. Whether the torments of absence were softened by a clandestine correspondence, let us not inquire. Mr. and Mrs. Morland never did--they had been too kind to exact any promise; and whenever Catherine received a letter, as, at that time, happened pretty often, they always looked another way.

The anxiety, which in this state of their attachment must be the portion of Henry and Catherine, and of all who loved either, as to its final event, can hardly extend, I fear, to the bosom of my readers, who will see in the tell-tale compression of the pages before them, that we are all hastening together to perfect felicity. The means by which their early marriage was effected can be the only doubt: what probable circumstance could work upon a temper like the general's? The circumstance which chiefly availed was the marriage of his (general) daughter with a man of fortune and consequence, which took place in the course of the summer--an accession of dignity that threw him (general) into a fit of good humour, from which he (general) did not recover till after Eleanor had obtained his (general) forgiveness of Henry, and his (general) permission for him (general)

``to be a fool if he (fool) liked it!'' (him)

The marriage of Eleanor Tilney, her (Tilney) removal from all the evils of such a home as Northanger had been made by Henry's banishment, to the home of her (Tilney) choice and the man of her (Tilney) choice, is an event which I expect to give general satisfaction among all her (Tilney) acquaintance. My own joy on the occasion is very sincere. I know no one more entitled, by unpretending merit, or better prepared by habitual suffering, to receive and enjoy felicity. Her (Tilney) partiality for this gentleman was not of recent origin; and he (gentleman) had been long withheld only by inferiority of situation from addressing her (Tilney). His (gentleman) unexpected accession to title and fortune had removed all his (gentleman) difficulties; and never had the general loved his (gentleman) daughter so well in all her (daughter) hours of companionship, utility, and patient endurance as when he (gentleman) first hailed her (daughter)

``Your Ladyship!'' (he)
Her (daughter) husband was really deserving of her (daughter); independent of his (gentleman) peerage, his (gentleman) wealth, and his (gentleman) attachment, being to a precision the most charming young man in the world. Any further definition of his (gentleman) merits must be unnecessary; the most charming young man in the world is instantly before the imagination of us all. Concerning the one in question, therefore, I have only to add--aware that the rules of composition forbid the introduction of a character not connected with my fable--that this was the very gentleman whose negligent servant left behind him (gentleman) that collection of washing-bills, resulting from a long visit at Northanger, by which my heroine was involved in one of her (heroine) most alarming adventures.

The influence of the viscount and viscountess in their brother's behalf was assisted by that right understanding of Mr. Morland's circumstances which, as soon as the general would allow himself to be informed, they were qualified to give. It taught him (Morland) that he (Morland) had been scarcely more misled by Thorpe's first boast of the family wealth than by his (Morland) subsequent malicious overthrow of it; that in no sense of the word were they necessitous or poor, and that Catherine would have three thousand pounds. This was so material an amendment of his (Morland) late expectations that it greatly contributed to smooth the descent of his (Morland) pride; and by no means without its effect was the private intelligence, which he (Morland) was at some pains to procure, that the Fullerton estate, being entirely at the disposal of its present proprietor, was consequently open to every greedy speculation.

On the strength of this, the general, soon after Eleanor's marriage, permitted his (proprietor) son to return to Northanger, and thence made him (proprietor) the bearer of his (proprietor) consent, very courteously worded in a page full of empty professions to Mr. Morland. The event which it authorized soon followed: Henry and Catherine were married, the bells rang, and everybody smiled; and, as this took place within a twelvemonth from the first day of their meeting, it will not appear, after all the dreadful delays occasioned by the general's cruelty, that they were essentially hurt by it. To begin perfect happiness at the respective ages of twenty-six and eighteen is to do pretty well; and professing myself moreover convinced that the general's unjust interference, so far from being really injurious to their felicity, was perhaps rather conducive to it, by improving their knowledge of each other, and adding strength to their attachment, I leave it to be settled, by whomsoever it may concern, whether the tendency of this work be altogether to recommend parental tyranny, or reward filial disobedience.

*Vide a letter from Mr. Richardson, No. 97, Vol. II, Rambler.

A NOTE ON THE TEXT

Northanger Abbey was written in 1797-98 under a different title. The manuscript was revised around 1803 and sold to a London publisher, Crosbie & Co., who sold it back in 1816. The Signet Classic text is based on the first edition, published by John Murray, London, in 1818--the year following Miss Austen's death. Spelling and punctuation have been largely brought into conformity with modern British usage.
